# 2\n
{London, British Library, Cotton Caligula A ix, The Owl and the Nightingale, language 1=}\n
C13b2\n
390 262 N\n
{=Central Worcs=}\n
{=Sample tagged: fols. 233r-239v line 13; 240r line 6-241v line 15,\n
i.e. all of language 1. The other sections of the Owl and the\n
Nightingale are in the same hand but in somewhat different language\n
which is placed (as text no 3) also in Worcs but a little to the\n
north-west. Other texts in this MS (tagged as nos. 238-244), also\n
in the same hand, are somewhat different again (though see Cartlidge\n
(1997) for orthographical groupings). These other texts have not been\n
given localisations=}\n
{=Script - skilled, professional book hand - Textura semi-quadrata=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=The scribe has the same somewhat variable shape for thorn,  wynn and\n
(rarely) Y. The variability is manifested in a cline of shapes,\n
clearly thorn-shaped and wynn-shaped at each end but often either\n
interchanged or indeterminate. Wynn and Y are almost always dotted,\n
thorn only very occasionally. I have noted when it is dotted. The\n
letters are transcribed as far as possible according to scribal\n
intention judged by context=}\n
{~f233ra~}\n
$/P11N_*ICH\n
{=Enlarged illuminated capital *I descending in the left margin to the\n
depth of six lines. A small guideletter I is visible to its left=}\n

$summer/naj<pr_SUMerE\n
{=I take this (as most editors do) as an attributive use of "summer"\n
rather than a feminine prepositional ending of "a certain" (< OE sum).\n
As the most recent editor, Cartlidge (2001), points out, a form of\n
"sum" does not naturally follow the indirect article ONE and OE dael\n
"dale" is normally neuter so should not attract a feminine adjective=}\n
$dale/n<pr{rh}_DALE\n

$/DatOd_yAT\n
$foul/ajOd_WOLE\n
{=I assume here that WOLE represents a form of the word FOUL which the\n
scribe elsewhere writes FUL[E]/WL[E] but cf. FOLE f233vb line 104\n
implying "foul, filthy". This form is equivalent to VUELE in J and the\n
word "evil" may be intended. X may have had VVELE with the first E looking\n
O-like. Later however C has VUEL[E]/UUEL. It is perhaps equally\n
possible that X had some form of FOUL which J has changed. A final\n
possibility is that the adjective FOL "foolish" (but also implying "evil"\n
in some recorded contexts) could be intended=}\n

$sooth/n<pr_SOzE\n
{=No need to emend to FOzE if we assume the exemplar had at least\n
occasional equivalence of yogh and thorn/wynn. That this was in\n
fact the case in the exemplar X is strongly suggested by the fact\n
that J and C sometimes differ in their interpretation of these letters\n
in their copies. The small cross-stroke on the initial letter observed\n
by Cartlidge (2001) is nothing like Scribe C\'s normal boldly formed\n
hasta and is merely a slightly smudged version of the normal\n
\'shoulders\' of the long S created by a splay of the pen when the first\n
minim stroke is made and before the top stroke is added. The letter is\n
S not F, though it must be remembered that this scribe does sometimes\n
write one for the other. An interpretation of rare FOzE from OE gefo:g\n
is therefore possible though perhaps less likely, especially with the\n
evidence of J\'s SOyE, and the fact that SOzE is a feasible spelling\n
for "sooth"=}\n

$seuen/vps13F_SU+y $/vps13[V]F_+y\n
{=The J scribe has ISYHy here, probably added to the text into a gap\n
presumably because he did not understand the exemplar form. As the C\n
scribe is a literatim copyist we can take it that the exemplar form was\n
probably SUy as here. This might be possible as a plural form of the\n
verb "see" though in this scribe\'s system one would expect SOy as indeed\n
he writes elsewhere, but it is not plausible as a form of the singular.\n
Cartlidge suggests that it is the ME version of the OF sivre (AN suer)\n
"follow" and I think this is the most likely reading here. "Who pursues\n
nothing to any good purpose" (See MED s.v. seuen v. (1) sense 4 (d)=}\n

$marsh/n<pr{rh}_MerSHE\n
{=Although "worse" and "marsh" do not provide a true rhyme, "marsh" is\n
confirmed by both J and C versions and is likely therefore to have been\n
the reading in the common exemplar. Whether it was the reading of the\n
original is not knowable, but as far as semantics are concerned, I\n
think it unnecessary to propose an otherwise unattested ME word from\n
an unattested OE *mae:rs "field boundaries" for this word (see Cartlidge\n
2001:114 following Kristensson and ultimately Stanley (for refs. see\n
Cartlidge). OE mersc is applied not only to permanent wetlands but also\n
to tidal marshes and to flood meadows that are not always inundated.\n
HAUECK is an unspecified "hawk" and could well imply a marsh harrier,\n
hen harrier or common buzzard all of which are frequently to be found\n
hunting over meadow and marshland. Sparrow hawk, Montagu\'s harrier and\n
peregrine falcon are also possibilities. Crows are to be found\n
anywhere where there are food sources for them, which would include\n
not only any kind of carrion to be found in marsh or meadow but also\n
the eggs and nestlings of meadow-nesting and marsh-nesting birds.\n
They would be just as likely to mob a hawk "by the marsh" as anywhere else=}\n
{.}\n

$tell/vps13_TEL+y $/vps13[L]_+y\n
{=Cartlidge (2001) seems to think that this form has to be from tae:lan but\n
this is semanatically unsatisfactory and is not supported, as he\n
suggests, by a similar phrase in Layamon with the preterite form TALDEN\n
because formally this also could equally well be from the verb "tell".\n
Forms with single L in 2nd and 3rd person present singular are\n
historically expected. What is perhaps unexpected at this date is that\n
double L has not been adopted in these persons by analogical levelling.\n
But here the contraction of the form seems to have blocked it. Cf. TELST\n
above and TELSTU below. The J version here has TELLEy=}\n

$awayward/av{rh}_A+wAI+wAR $a-/xp-av_A+ $-ward/xs-av{rh}_+wAR\n
{=MS AwAIwART - but It seems to me that a later hand has added\n
the final T, partially obscuring the main scribe\'s end-of-line\n
punctus. The final T has a longer top stroke than the main\n
scribe\'s usual T does and its stem is angled forward. Editors\n
have not, however, noted this as a later correction. Both Atkins\n
(1922) and Wells (1907) silently emend to AWAI-WARD. Stanley\n
(1960) and Cartlidge (2001) read AWAIWART with no comment. For\n
another example of the C scribe omitting final dental see HOR\n
below, also corrected (to HORD) by a different hand=}\n

$/P13GM_IS\n
$tongue/nOd_TUNGE\n
{=Presumably with the sense "prick, male organ". A later corrector has\n
added S between IS and TUNGE to make ISSTUNGE. On the basis of this,\n
most editors read ISSTUNGE, as past participle of "sting" with the sense\n
"penetrated". And the Jesus version (also IS TUNGE) is read as simply\n
ISTUNGE. It seems to me that ISSTUNGE, with double S, is not itself a\n
plausible form for the past participle. In fact it seems to me more\n
likely that the corrector was trying to change one noun ("tongue") to\n
another ("sting"). Cartlidge (2001) reports the MS readings accurately but\n
accepts the reading ISTUNGE "stung". Only Wells (1906) suggests the\n
possibility of reading "tongue" for "pudendum". He also surmises that\n
the common original had either IS TUNGE or ISTUNGE (for "stung").\n
Whatever the author\'s intention, here it is the scribal text that\n
must be interpreted and tagged accordingly=}\n
$under/pr_UNDER\n
$ga:ra/n<pr{rh}_GORE\n
{.}\n

$/P21GD_UNKer\n
{=The abbreviation here is the same shape as that used for <er> in other\n
words, a 7-shaped hook. Usually the abbreviation is above the preceding\n
letter. Here it is beside the K and the hairline tail is lengthened\n
through the limb of the K. Here I expand as <er> because of the shape.\n
Elsewhere the sequence <ker> is abbreviated as K with a simple stroke\n
through the limb of the K which I realise as Kx=}\n

$in{c}/pr_ON\n
$thin?/n<pr{rh}_yINNE\n
{="in straits, having a thin time?", see Stanley (1960). The first\n
letter is clearly thorn-like but could of course be read wynn in this\n
hand. This is what the editors, including Stanley, in fact do. The\n
translation would then be something like "when the heart is in strife,\n
agitated". Against this reading is the Jesus reading which is also\n
yINNE. The J-scribe does not use a separate wynn rune but on the few\n
occasions when he fails to alter exemplar wynn to W he adopts a Y-shaped\n
dotted character not a thorn. This suggests that the exemplar had clear thorn\n
not wynn. In these circumstances I take it the C-scribe who was a\n
literatim copyist intended the sequence yINNE here=}\n
{.}\n

$all/av_AL+\n
$tosli:tan/vpp{rh}_+TO-SLIT\n
{=This form suggests that the original strong verb may by this stage\n
have become weak. The MS reading is clearly divided ALTO SLIT (the J\n
version is divided evenly AL TO SLIT). In the light of the C version, it\n
might seem more logical therefore to tag this phrase "alltoo slit". But\n
the C scribe very frequently runs adverbial "to" together with the\n
following word or morph and adverbial "alltoo" seems never to be used\n
with verbal forms, even past participles functioning as adjectives. So\n
here I follow all previous editors in assuming the past participle to be\n
from OE tosli:tan "tear asunder" rather than the simplex sli:tan=}\n

$fese/nOd{rh}_wES\n
{=This means "rush, onslaught, blast". It is a new reading (see Laing\n
(2001:108-117). Cartlidge (2001:122) does not accept the reading from a\n
prepublication suggestion, but had not seen the published argument. In\n
my opinion, however, this makes better sense than Stanley\'s and\n
Cartlidge\'s reading, yES "of this", which is the more suspect as\n
the J scribe had difficulty with whatever was in the exemplar and\n
failed to produce a reading at all here. A later scribe substituted\n
BLES "blast". The first letter is wynn-shaped but undotted so could\n
be read wynn or thorn=}\n
{.}\n

$fox/n_FOR\n
{=sic - error for FOX. J version also has FOR. The mistake\n
probably arises from the fact that in some formal scripts\n
the first element of X is identical to that of R and the\n
second stroke, crossing the first obliquely from top right to bottom\n
left, can also be identical at its top end to the limb of R. If the\n
cross-stroke is kept short, without an extravagant descender, the two\n
figurae can look very similar indeed. It is interesting that in the\n
case of FOR for "fox" both the J and the C scribe substituted 2-shaped\n
R, usual in their scripts after O, for what is likely to have been X\n
mistaken for the straight short R used in other contexts=}\n
$so/av_SO\n

$both/aj_BO\n
$/P12GNpl+V_yIN\n
{=y corrected from z by overwriting. Cartlidge (2001:126) suggests that\n
the correction is from zIM to yIN. But this assumes that the C scribe\n
does not correct as he goes along whereas I think that the evidence\n
shows that he often does so, noticing his mistake straight away before\n
he has finished writing the word. This is another example that suggests\n
the exemplar scribe had litteral equivalence of thorn/wynn and yogh. I\n
believe the C scribe saw zIN and copied it literatim, realised that in\n
his system this produced a non-word, recognised the yogh/thorn\n
interchange, overwrote yogh and the first minim with thorn and then\n
added the necessary extra minim to produce yIN before copying the next\n
word=}\n
$eye/npl{rh}_EzE\n

$/TN_yE\n
$knight/n_KINzT\n
{=sic - there is clear differentiation by serifs of the first two minims\n
and clear linkage of the second two. Cf the word IN in the line below.\n
There is also an oblique stroke on the first minim. The stroke seems to\n
have been made twice, once almost vertical hitting the descender of yogh\n
above it and then repeated somewhat to the right but still clearly\n
pointing at the first minim not at the second and certainly avoiding the\n
third. All editors simply read KNIzT, but the KINzT spelling is clear\n
and moreover is almost certainly a genuine form not an error. Cf.\n
similar spellings (presumably implying metathesis perhaps via an epenthetic\n
vowel between [k] and [n]) in Layamon A hand B, the Caius version of AR and\n
the Lambeth Homilies. Also see MED s.v. knight n. for many other\n
citations, and for full discussion see Laing & Lass (2005). These\n
spellings may indeed have been commoner still, but have been disguised\n
by the fact that in many scripts <ni> and <in> are virtually\n
indistinguishable so that there are few unequivocal <in> readings in\n
this or other <kni-> words and editors have therefore been less likely\n
to notice those there are. Note, for instance, in Havelok for\n
"knight(s)" four examples of KINCTES, two each of KINTH and KINCTH and\n
one each of KINCT and KINTHES=}\n
$be/vpt13_wAS\n
$with{a}/pr_wIy\n
$/P11<pr_ME\n
$wroth/aj{rh}_wROy\n

\n
\\# 3\n
{London, British Library, Cotton Caligula A ix, The Owl and the Nightingale, language 2}\n
C13b2\n
379 267 W\n
{=NW Worcs=}\n
{=Sample tagged: fols. 239v line 14-240r line 5; 241v line 16-246r, i.e.\n
all of language 2. The other sections of The Owl and the Nightingale\n
are in the same hand but in somewhat different language which is\n
placed (as text no 2) also in Worcs but a little to the south-east.\n
Other texts in this MS (tagged as nos. 238-244), also in the same\n
hand, are somewhat different again (though see Cartlidge (1997) for\n
orthographical groupings). These other texts have not been given\n
localisations=}\n
{=Script - skilled, professional book hand - Textura semiquadrata=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=The scribe has the same somewhat variable shape for thorn, wynn and\n
(rarely) Y. The variability is manifested in a cline of shapes,\n
clearly thorn-shaped and wynn-shaped at each end but often either\n
interchanged or indeterminate. Wynn and Y are almost always dotted,\n
thorn only very occasionally. I have noted when it is dotted. The\n
letters are transcribed as far as possible according to scribal\n
intention judged by context. Note that the abbrevation for yer\n
"there" and y~ "that" (not used in language 1) are identical in\n
this hand. See John Scahill, \'Abbreviations in the Orthographies\n
of The Owl and the Nightingale and their Textual Implications\',\n
Notes and Queries 240 (1995), 426-8. Since the abbreviation mark\n
not only does not go through the ascender of thorn as y~ would\n
normally imply, but does not even touch it, I have realised the\n
forms for both "that" and "there" as yer. Note that a similar\n
abbreviation in hand A of Royal 17.A.xxvii does touch the ascender\n
(though does not bisect it) and does not appear to be used for\n
"there". In Royal the line indicating d rather than D similarly\n
attaches to the right of the ascender rather than bisecting it.\n
In that MS I therefore use y~ to indicate the abbrevated form of\n
"that" although it might be argued that the shape is hardly\n
different from the C scribe\'s form transcribed as yer. (Cf. a\n
similar situation in hand B of Layamon A.) Compare also the\n
abbreviation for "through" found here, as thorn with a line\n
through the descender. This abbreviation is found also in the work of\n
the  Worcester Tremulous Hand, and in BL Royal 17 A xxvii, hands A, B\n
and C and in BL Cotton Nero A. xiv both hands=}\n
{~f239va line14~}\n
{=line 901=}\n
$/neg-v(>nN)_*NE\n
$we:nan/vps13_wEN+ED $/vps13[N]_+ED\n
$noman/n_NA-MAN $man/n-k_-MAN\n
$for/pr_FOR\n
$/P12G<pr+C_yI\n
$pipe/vn<prK2{rh}_PIP+INGE $/vn<prK2{rh}_+INGE\n
{.}\n
{=Punctus almost covered by capital thorn in the second column=}\n
{\\}\n
$that/cj_yer+\n
$any/aj_+EN+I $-ig/xs-aj_+I\n
$priest/n_PREOST\n
$in{p}/pr_IN\n
$church/n<pr_CHIRGE\n
{=The C scribe originally wrote CHIRCE. Before writing the next word he\n
changed CE to G by allowing C and the back of E to serve as the body of\n
G and adding the second element (descender) of G below. The stroke\n
that links the head of G to the next letter is then drawn across the\n
top of original E, further to disguise its existence, and a second E\n
added to make CHIRGE. It is quite clear that this is the order of\n
events not that G has been corrected to C because the pen angles\n
observable in the body of G are those used for the formation of\n
C not of G in this hand. G for more usual CH seems to be a minor\n
SWML variant. Note also HEOUERIGE "kingdom of heaven" in the C\n
scribe\'s copy of Doomsday (text no. 241) and forms with G rather\n
than CH in "each" and "I" (Cambridge Trinity College 323 hand A,\n
text no. 246) and in "ditch", "each" and "such" (hand D, text no.\n
249). Cf. a number of G spellings for CH as well as GCH for C(H)CH\n
in BL Royal 2.F.viii, fol. 1v (text no. 263) and CHERGE and CHURGE\n
for "church" recorded in LALME in Somerset and Oxon respectively=}\n

$/P13NF_HEO\n
$par/pr_Par\n
{=MS has P with a line through the descender. Par has been subpuncted\n
and FOR inserted in the left margin by a later hand. P with a line\n
through the descender is the usual abbreviation for "par" with the\n
sense "by, for", confined in Middle English to phrases from French\n
or Latin (see MED s.v. par prep.) In the LAEME corpus the only\n
examples of its use are in the phrase "par (seint) charite". It\n
seems likely therefore that the C scribe\'s use of this preposition\n
here is a textual error, whether transmitted from the exemplar or\n
not. The J version has FOR. The simplest explanation is that this\n
is a temporary lapse into Latin or French either by the C scribe\n
or by the exemplar scribe whose lapse was corrected by the J scribe.\n
See Cartlidge (2001:132) and the reference there cited for the\n
different explanation that Par is a misreading of the exemplar\'s\n
abbreviation for "through". Both Wells (1907) and Atkins (1922)\n
read yurh with no further comment, but the P is quite clear. I\n
think exemplar abbreviated "through" is unlikely given the J\n
scribe\'s FOR and the fact that the J scribe elsewhere deciphers\n
exemplar "through" correctly. Whether or not the exemplar reading\n
was intended to be "through" the C scribe has here written Par\n
which he presumably meant to have the sense "by, for"=}\n

$/P11Oi_ME+\n
$sooth/av_+SOy\n
{=MS looks like MESSOy, with the second S a clumsy correction from what\n
was probably D to a long version of 8-shaped S. There is no ascender of\n
original D visible but its lobe shows biting with the following O. I\n
think what happened was this: the scribe first wrote ME DOy,\n
recognized his mistake, tried to alter the D to S by overwriting, was\n
dissatisfied and added a normal long-S in the space between ME and the\n
botched correction. It might be argued that the C scribe does not\n
normally leave so much of a gap between words and it is true that the\n
long-S shows no sign of being squashed into a space too small. But\n
occasionally there are larger gaps between words, one clear example\n
is four lines up from here in the left hand column between GODE and\n
Gr^Iy. The accident of having a larger gap than usual available could\n
have contributed to the scribe\'s decision to make the double\n
correction as he did=}\n

{=This reading combines loss of initial <h> (not common but paralleled\n
in scribe C\'s usage, see ATTEST fol. 234vb in language A); paragogic\n
<e> (cf. HAREM fol. 242ra line 1260) and final N for M (cf. *SUN "some"\n
fol. 243rb; wISDON "wisdom" fol. 243vb, *FRON "from" fol. 244vb). The\n
combination itself is not paralleled elsewhere but is formally possible\n
within the writing system and gives the required sense perfectly. This\n
spelling (presumably that of the exemplar) seems, however, to have\n
thrown the J scribe who paraphrases here=}\n

$bethink/vps13_BI-yEN+Ey $be-/xp-v_BI- $/vps13[N]_+Ey\n
{=sic - Cartlidge glosses this correctly as "thinks about", but reads\n
BI-wENEy although the letter after B is undotted and more thorn-like\n
than wynn-like. He takes  the word to be from OE bewenian which in\n
fact  means "entertain, be host to" and is not the sense required\n
here. The correct meaning might be supplied by bi- + we:nan but this\n
seems not to be attested elsewhere. In view of the J version\'s\n
BI-yENKy, which gives exactly the sense required in a commonly used\n
word, it seems most likely that the C scribe has omitted CH by\n
mistake or that the second E is an error for C=}  \n

$waste/ajpl-av_wEST+E $/plaj-av_+E\n
!_L~\n
{=& is omitted and L with the usual abbreviation for <er> written\n
instead. Cartlidge (2001: 136) conjectures that the C scribe began to\n
right LERE with the abbreviation - Ler - and then copied the full word\n
without erasing the previous attempt. This, however, does not account\n
for missing "and". L with a hook or line through is the usual\n
abbreviation for Latin "vel". The Tironian sign is common to both\n
Latin and vernacular scripts standing for "et" in Latin and French\n
and "and" in English. I think L\' here is the accidental substitution\n
of the C scribe of one common Latin abbreviation for another. Cf. Par\n
for "for" above=}\n

$shroud/vpp_I+SCHUD $ge-/xp-vpp_I+\n
{=Missing R is perhaps as a result of an exemplar spelling in ISCHRID,\n
see MED s.v. shri:den v. Cartlidge (2001:136) suggests the form is for\n
"shod" but U for O is not a usual substitution in this scribe\'s system\n
and in light of the J version\'s ISRUD and the following line,\n
"clothed" seems more likely=}\n

$under/pr_UNDER\n
!_yEO-LINE\n
{=The J version here has VNDER WELKNE which clearly preserves the\n
correct sense. The C scribe\'s version is presumably misread from\n
exemplar wEOLCNE but as it stands it is hard to see what the scribe\n
thought he was writing other than nonsense. yEO is his form for "those"\n
and could conceivably be stretched to stand for singular "the". There is a\n
stock phrase: under li:n "under linen" meaning "clothed in linen", i.e "all\n
men" (see MED li(:)n n. sense 3 (b)). It is just conceivable that this\n
is what the C scribe was thinking of but it clearly makes no sense here=}\n

{\\}\n
$scill/av_*SCHILLE\n
{="shrilly"? This is probably an error for SCHULE, cf. J\'s version,\n
*SCHULLE where the verb is present first singular subjunctive in form:\n
"If I were to raise a hue and cry upon you". This gives better sense\n
than "Shrilly I raise a hue and cry upon you" and is almost certainly\n
what was intended. But forms of "shall" with medial -I- are otherwise\n
unattested in early ME and are very rare in late ME. I think it\n
likely that the C scribe miscopied SCHULE from the exemplar. Note\n
that in Language 1 plural and subjunctive forms of "shall" are spelled\n
SHULLE, but here in language 2 plural forms are spelled SCHULE. If,\n
as seems likely, the exemplar had SCHULE here, C\'s reading would only\n
require that he mistake the second minim of U for L. I assume he took\n
the word to be an adverbial form of "shrill" from OE scill, which\n
appears with this spelling, three times as an adjective and once\n
adverbially, elsewhere in his copy. This reading is favoured by Wells\n
(1907:181), though he does suggest it may be a slip for SCHULLE. Other\n
editors accept the -I- spelling for a form of "shall" without question.\n
It is perhaps a possible spelling in early Middle English although\n
apparently not elsewhere attested at this date. OE forms with umlaut,\n
scyle, seem to have given rise (see OED) to sciele and scile, though\n
only one example of the latter is cited from Aelfric. In the SWML\n
one would expect \'u\' spellings and judging from the other forms in\n
the C\'s scribe\'s usage I think SCHILLE an implausible spelling within\n
his system; so I tentatively tag the word here as a form of OE scill=}\n
